        gptSubPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.subPage ? digitalData.page.subPage : "blank";

        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

        googletag.pubads()
            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send
            .setTargeting('doi', gptClean('10.1002/smll.201502278')) // Publication DOI
            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

        googletag.defineSlot($GooglePublisherCategory, 'fluid', 'advert-leaderboard')
            .defineSizeMapping(sizes.leaderboard)
            .addService(googletag.pubads())
            .setCollapseEmptyDiv(true);

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="3cb9532b86264fa4bca68c3c87979936"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="3cb9532b86264fa4bca68c3c87979936">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '854df47a-579b-408b-9e82-68aea919d6cb'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

        (function() {
            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {
                var store_domain = 'https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com';
                var session_id = 'D3D13FA64C188119AC3B4CB6604092FD';
                var url = ('https:' == document.location.protocol ? 'https://' : 'http://')
                    + "beacon.riskified.com?shop=" + store_domain + "&sid=" + session_id;

                s.type = 'text/javascript';
                s.async = true;
                s.src = url;
                var x = document.getElementsByTagName('script')[0];
                x.parentNode.insertBefore(s, x);
            }
            if (window.attachEvent)
                window.attachEvent('onload', riskifiedBeaconLoad)
            else
                window.addEventListener('load', riskifiedBeaconLoad, false);
        })();

China</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Cao/Yuliang">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Ai/Xinping" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a4" data-db-target-for="a4" aria-controls="a4" aria-haspopup="true" id="a4_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Xinping Ai</span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a4" aria-labelledby="a4_Ctrl" role="region" id="a4">
                     <p class="author-name">Xinping Ai</p>
                     <p>College of Chemistry and Molecular Science, Wuhan University, Wuhan, 430072 P. R. China</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Ai/Xinping">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Yang/Hanxi" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a5" data-db-target-for="a5" aria-controls="a5" aria-haspopup="true" id="a5_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Hanxi Yang<i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-mail_outline"></i></span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a5" aria-labelledby="a5_Ctrl" role="region" id="a5">
                     <p class="author-type mb-1">Corresponding Author</p>
                     <p class="author-name">Hanxi Yang</p>
                     <p>College of Chemistry and Molecular Science, Wuhan University, Wuhan, 430072 P. R. China</p>E-mail: <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:jfqian@whu.edu.cn"><span>jfqian@whu.edu.cn</span></a>, <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:hxyang@whu.edu.cn"><span>hxyang@whu.edu.cn</span></a><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Yang/Hanxi">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span></div>
         </div>
      </div>
   </div>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en">
         <h2 id="d56705431" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p><b>Graphene-wrapped organic nanoflowers</b> are synthesized from ultrasonic treatment of a simple microsized disodium salt (Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>12</sub>H<sub>6</sub>O<sub>4</sub>) and graphene, which demonstrates a greatly enhanced electrochemical capacity, rate capability and cycling stability as organic Na<sup>+</sup> storage anode. This work suggests an effective architecture to make organic materials electrochemically energetic and stable for energy storage applications.

            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a019aa5d-040b-41ea-aa16-d931073b20fe/smll201502278-gra-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/a019aa5d-040b-41ea-aa16-d931073b20fe/smll201502278-gra-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/fdcb87cd-0e28-4ab7-84f5-19d2deead3c1/smll201502278-gra-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a019aa5d-040b-41ea-aa16-d931073b20fe/smll201502278-gra-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll201502278-sec-0010">
         
         <p>Effective utilization of renewable energy sources such as solar and wind electricity requires affordable and efficient electric storage technologies, which can integrate intermittent renewable powers into the electric grid. Despite many types of rechargeable batteries, from conventional lead-acid to the advanced Li-ion batteries, have been tried for electric energy storage, all of them are difficult to meet the cost and material requirements for grid-scale applications.<span><a href="#smll201502278-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201502278-bib-0001_R_d56705321e292" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Science Chem. Rev.">1</a></span> As a new alternative to present rechargeable batteries, Na-ion batteries are recently reconsidered to be an attractive candidate for large-scale electric storage applications because of their electrochemical similarity to Li-ion technology, low cost and abundance of Na resources.<span><a href="#smll201502278-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201502278-bib-0002_R_d56705321e294" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Energy Environ. Sci. Energy Environ. Sci. Adv. Func. Mater.">2</a></span> However, the Na-host materials developed so far are mostly based on transition metal compounds,<span><a href="#smll201502278-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201502278-bib-0003_R_d56705321e296" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Energy Environ. Sci. Adv. Energy Mater. Chem. Commun.">3</a></span> which would still cause cost and environmental concerns for large-scale applications. To overcome these concerns, recent development of new Na-storage materials shifts from transition-metal compounds to “metalfree” organic hosts with the aim to build sustainable and environmentally benign Na-ion batteries.<span><a href="#smll201502278-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201502278-bib-0004_R_d56705321e298" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Energy Environ. Sci.">4</a></span></p>
         
         <p>In the search for organic Na-storage materials, a number of redox-active polymers, such as p-type conducting polymers<span><a href="#smll201502278-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201502278-bib-0005_R_d56705321e302" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Electrochem. Commun. Adv. Mater. Chem. Commun.">5</a></span> and aromatic carbonyl polyimides,<span><a href="#smll201502278-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201502278-bib-0006_R_d56705321e304" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Energy Mater. J. Mater. Chem. A">6</a></span> have been developed to demonstrate high capacities and cycling stability as organic cathodes. However, few of the redox polymers are reported as organic anodes,<span><a href="#smll201502278-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201502278-bib-0007_R_d56705321e306" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Sci. Rep. Nat. Commun.">7</a></span> probably due to the difficulty to find an n-type redox polymer with acceptable Na-storage capability at sufficiently low potential. Recently, a number of small aromatic carboxylates were found to undergo their redox reaction through an electrochemical enolization mechanism with Na<sup>+</sup> ions associating with and disassociating from their carbonyl groups at quite low potentials (&lt;0.5 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na), performing as anode materials for Na-ion batteries.<span><a href="#smll201502278-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201502278-bib-0008_R_d56705321e313" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Energy &amp; Environ. Sci. Adv. Energy Mater.">8</a>-<a href="#smll201502278-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201502278-bib-0010_R_d56705321e313" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">10</a></span> Almost at the same time, several research groups reported reversible Na<sup>+</sup> insertion behavior of sodium benzene-dicarboxylate, Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>8</sub>H<sub>4</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, which exhibited a nearly two Na storage capacity of ≈300 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> as organic anodes.<span><a href="#smll201502278-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201502278-bib-0008_R_d56705321e328" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Energy &amp; Environ. Sci. Adv. Energy Mater.">8</a>, <a href="#smll201502278-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201502278-bib-0009_R_d56705321e328" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Mater.">9</a></span> Later, Chen co-workers<span><a href="#smll201502278-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201502278-bib-0011_R_d56705321e330" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Angew. Chem.">11</a></span> disclosed a bipolar organic tetrasodium salt, Na<sub>4</sub>C<sub>8</sub>H<sub>2</sub>O<sub>6</sub>, which can either serve as a cathode at 2.4 V or an anode at 0.4 V to deliver a reversible capacity of ≈180 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Very recently, Wang et al.<span><a href="#smll201502278-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201502278-bib-0010_R_d56705321e343" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">10</a></span> designed a sodium 4,4′-stilbene-dicarboxylate molecule with extended π-conjugated system that demonstrated fast charge/discharge capabilities. Because of the poor electronic conductivity of the organic molecular solids, these organic anodes have to be made with the addition of 40%–50% conductive carbon, which leads to a huge sacrifice of the volumetric/gravimetric energy densities of the electrodes.</p>
         
         <p>To promote the electrochemical utilization of the electronically nonconductive organic compounds, an effective strategy is to downsize and to disperse the organic materials in conductive matrix, thus creating abundant electronic and ionic channels for electron transfer reaction. Inspired from the nanoarchitecture design of inorganic insertion anodes for improving Na-storage performances,<span><a href="#smll201502278-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201502278-bib-0012_R_d56705321e348" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Chem. Mater.">12</a></span> we develop a new 3D porous architecture of organic/graphene nanocomposite, where electrode-active organic compounds are assembled into hierarchical porous nanospheres wrapped in graphene nanosheets, thus enabling them electronically and ionically accessible for redox reaction. In this work, we selected a simple organic disodium salt, sodium naphthalene dicarboxylate (Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>12</sub>H<sub>6</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, denoted as Na<sub>2</sub>NC), as a Na-host skeleton and synthesized Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>12</sub>H<sub>6</sub>O<sub>4</sub> nanoflowers (NF-Na<sub>2</sub>NC) and then prepared graphene-wrapped NF-Na<sub>2</sub>NC nanocomposite (Na<sub>2</sub>NC/G) as an organic anode material for Na-ion batteries. Benefiting from its large surface areas of the NF-Na<sub>2</sub>NC nanoflowers and fast electronic conduction of the graphene framework, the Na<sub>2</sub>NC/G anode demonstrates a remarkable high capacity of 226 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, a strong rate capability at 10 C rate and good cycle stability.</p>
         
         <p>The synthetic processes of Na<sub>2</sub>NC/G nanocomposite were schematically depicted in Scheme S1 (Supporting Information). The raw Na<sub>2</sub>NC material was synthesized through an acid-base reaction of 2,6-naphthalene dicarboxylic acid with NaOH. The as-prepared Na<sub>2</sub>NC powders were ultrasonically exfoliated and reassembled in methanol to form flower-shaped nanoparticles. Then, these nanoparticles were dispersed into graphene nanosheets in methanol to give the Na<sub>2</sub>NC/G material. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll201502278-fig-0001">1</a> shows the morphological changes of Na<sub>2</sub>NC powders during the ultrasonic treatment. As shown in the transmission electron microscope (TEM) image, the as-prepared Na<sub>2</sub>NC powders appeared as microfibres of 0.5–1 μm diameters (MF-Na<sub>2</sub>NC, Figure <a href="#smll201502278-fig-0001">1</a>a). After ultrasonic treatment for 10–20 min in methanol, the Na<sub>2</sub>NC fibres were first exfoliated into aggregated nanoparticles (Figure <a href="#smll201502278-fig-0001">1</a>b) and then reassembled into pompon-like spheres of ≈1 μm diameter (Figure <a href="#smll201502278-fig-0001">1</a>c). When the ultrasonic treatment lasted for 30 min, all the Na<sub>2</sub>NC particles were finally assembled into porous hollow spheres (Figure <a href="#smll201502278-fig-0001">1</a>d). The formation process from microrods to nanospheres can be well explained by the Ostwald Ripening mechanism.<span><a href="#smll201502278-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201502278-bib-0013_R_d56705321e418" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - J. Am. Ceramic Soc.">13</a></span> Figure <a href="#smll201502278-fig-0001">1</a>e clearly shows the structure and morphology of the Na<sub>2</sub>NC/G nanocomposite, where the porous Na<sub>2</sub>NC spheres are well-dispersed and wrapped in few layers of graphene nanosheets that provide not only a conducting network for electron transport but also an effective shield to prevent the aggregation of the Na<sub>2</sub>NC particles. The scanning electron microscope (SEM) image in Figure <a href="#smll201502278-fig-0001">1</a>f reveals that each NF-Na<sub>2</sub>NC sphere consists of a large number of interconnected nanosheets that form a porous hierarchical structure. There is no doubt that the abundant mesopores and gullies in the NF-Na<sub>2</sub>NC nanoflowers provide sufficient ionic channels for the electrolyte to establish large accessible internal surfaces for electrochemical redox reaction of the organic molecules. The SEM and TEM images of Na<sub>2</sub>NC and Na<sub>2</sub>NC/G samples in a wider range are given in Figure S1 of the Supporting Information, which show the homogeneity of these materials.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll201502278-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/2303489e-8947-4d1c-bece-b8cc183b9259/smll201502278-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/2303489e-8947-4d1c-bece-b8cc183b9259/smll201502278-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a3905740-1837-43ce-95ca-9c93d8b65965/smll201502278-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/2303489e-8947-4d1c-bece-b8cc183b9259/smll201502278-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201502278-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201502278" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">TEM images of the as-prepared Na<sub>2</sub>NC powders with an ultrasonic treatment time of a) 0 min.(MF-Na<sub>2</sub>NC); b) 10 min; c) 20 min; d) 30 min; e) TEM image of the Na<sub>2</sub>NC/G nanocomposite; and f) a SEM image of the NF-Na<sub>2</sub>NC nanoflowers.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Infrared analysis (Figure S2, Supporting Information) ensures the correct chemical structure of the as-prepared MF-Na<sub>2</sub>NC fibers and the identical chemical composition of the NF-Na<sub>2</sub>NC nanoflowers alone or in graphene nanosheets, suggesting that the ultrasonic exfoliation and reassembly processes did not induce any chemical change in the synthesis of Na<sub>2</sub>NC/G nanocomposite. <sup>1</sup>H NMR and <sup>13</sup>C NMR spectra (Figure S3, Supporting Information) show sharp singlet signals from the carboxylate groups or from the naphthyl groups, further confirming a pure phase of Na<sub>2</sub>NC crystal. Thermogravimetric tests (Figure S4, Supporting Information) reveal that the as-prepared Na<sub>2</sub>NC sample can be thermally stable up to 400 °C in N<sub>2</sub> atmosphere, which suggests a good thermal stability of this organic material for battery applications. Nitrogen adsorption–desorption isotherms and Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) surface area analysis reveal a great difference in the pore structure and surface area between the MF-Na<sub>2</sub>NC and NF-Na<sub>2</sub>NC samples. As shown in Figure S5 of the Supporting Information, the MF-Na<sub>2</sub>NC fibres exhibit a type II isotherm that is characteristic of a macroporous material, while the Na<sub>2</sub>NC nanoflowers give a type IV isotherm, showing a mesoporous structure. Benefiting from this mesoporous structure, the MF-Na<sub>2</sub>NC nanoflowers exhibit a great increase in their BET surface area (21.00 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>) and pore volume (0.12 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>) accordingly. When wrapped in graphene nanosheets, the resulting Na<sub>2</sub>NC/G nanocomposite gives a further increased BET surface area of 40.26 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> and pore volume of 0.16 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>, which ensures a better electrochemical utilization of this organic material.</p>
         
         <p>The electrochemical Na-storage properties of the Na<sub>2</sub>NC/G material were comparatively investigated with those of MF-Na<sub>2</sub>NC and NF-Na<sub>2</sub>NC samples. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll201502278-fig-0002">2</a>a shows the cyclic voltammgrams (CV) from the three types of organic Na<sub>2</sub>NC anodes. Apparently, the MF-Na<sub>2</sub>NC anode gives a pair of weak and broad anodic/cathodic bands in the low potential region of 0.8–0 V, whereas the NF-Na<sub>2</sub>NC anode exhibit a pair of much larger and sharper cathodic/anodic peaks at 0.25/0.55 V, respectively, reflecting a much higher electrochemical activity of the Na<sub>2</sub>NC nanoflowers than the Na<sub>2</sub>NC microfibers. In contrast, the Na<sub>2</sub>NC/G anode demonstrates a pair of even stronger redox peaks with a narrower interval between the anodic and cathodic peak potentials, which suggests an increased electrochemical kinetics due to the increased electronic conductivity and active reaction areas in the presence of a highly conductive graphene network.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll201502278-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/8a3966b6-e6d9-48e4-85a9-22e43cde7917/smll201502278-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/8a3966b6-e6d9-48e4-85a9-22e43cde7917/smll201502278-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ee82d8b2-3f8e-4760-9514-0196c63c8b2a/smll201502278-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/8a3966b6-e6d9-48e4-85a9-22e43cde7917/smll201502278-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201502278-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201502278" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance characterization of the three types of Na<sub>2</sub>NC anodes (MF-Na<sub>2</sub>NC, NF-Na<sub>2</sub>NC and Na<sub>2</sub>NC/G): a) The CV curves at a scan rate of 0.05 mV s<sup>−1</sup>; b) Charge/discharge profiles at a constant current of 10 mA g<sup>−1</sup>; c) Rate capability at various current rates as labeled on the curves; d) Cycling performance at 0.1 C rate (1 C = 200 mA g<sup>−1</sup>). All the data were taken from the second cycle.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Figure <a href="#smll201502278-fig-0002">2</a>b shows stable charge/discharge profiles of these Na<sub>2</sub>NC materials in Na<sup>+</sup> electrolyte at the second cycle and the first charge/discharge curves of these anodes are given in Figure S6 of the Supporting Information. In a good accord with their CV features, all the three different morphological Na<sub>2</sub>NC materials exhibit a discharge plateau at 0.3–0.4 V and a charge plateau at 0.5–0.6 V, respectively, which are in a very appropriate potential region for Na storage anodes. In comparison, the MF-Na<sub>2</sub>NC fibres give charge/discharge capacity of 136.4/126.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, while the Na<sub>2</sub>NC nanoflowers can deliver 30% higher charge/discharge capacity of 179.5/164.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at the similar potentials. Very impressively, the Na<sub>2</sub>NC/G composite demonstrates high discharge/charge capacities of 240.3/225.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, which are even higher than the theoretical 2e<sup>−</sup> redox capacity (206 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) of Na<sub>2</sub>NC compound. If taking into account of the volumetric energy density, the Na<sub>2</sub>NC/G electrode demonstrated a volumetric capacity of 90 mAh cm<sup>−3</sup>, which is quite high for organic materials. Considering that graphene sheets and conductive SP have a certain ability to host Na<sup>+</sup> ions at the potential region between 0 and 1.0 V,<span><a href="#smll201502278-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201502278-bib-0009_R_d56705321e610" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Mater.">9</a>, <a href="#smll201502278-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201502278-bib-0014_R_d56705321e610" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - ACS Nano Adv. Mater. Adv. Func. Mater. J. Phys. Chem. C">14</a></span> we have also measured the Na-storage capacity of a pure SP/graphene electrode (Figure S7, Supporting Information) and found that its capacity contribution to the Na<sub>2</sub>NC/G anode is only ≈20 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. Therefore, the major contribution of the graphene and SP network is to provide improved electronic conduction that promotes the Na-storage reaction.</p>
         
         <p>In addition to its improved high capacity, the Na<sub>2</sub>NC/G composite also exhibits significant improvement in the rate capability and cycling stability. As displayed in Figure <a href="#smll201502278-fig-0002">2</a>c, with increasing current density to 1 C rate, the MF-Na<sub>2</sub>NC anode can hardly deliver any discernible capacity, while the NF-Na<sub>2</sub>NC anode still exhibit a quite high capacity of 118 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. Nevertheless, when the current rate changed from 0.1 C to various high rates, the Na<sub>2</sub>NC/G anode can still deliver a considerably high capacity of 185, 118, and 88 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 1, 5, and 10 C rate, respectively, demonstrating a much stronger high-rate capability than its analogues. Such a high-rate performance of the Na<sub>2</sub>NC/G anode is apparently resulted from its greatly reduced electrochemical impedance. As shown in Figure S8 of the Supporting Information, the electrochemical impedances of the Na<sub>2</sub>NC/G anode is 470 Ω, which is only a quarter of the value of the MF-Na<sub>2</sub>NC anode (2350 Ω) and a half of the value of the NF-Na<sub>2</sub>NC anodes (1000 Ω). The greatly reduced impedance of the Na<sub>2</sub>NC/G anode is obviously due to its porous 3D structure with abundant reaction areas and ionic transport channels. Similarly, the Na<sub>2</sub>NC/G anode also demonstrates a greatly extended cycle life. As displayed in Figure <a href="#smll201502278-fig-0002">2</a>d, the reversible capacity of the MF-Na<sub>2</sub>NC anode quickly drops about 80% during 40 cycles, while the NF-Na<sub>2</sub>NC anode remains 89% of its capacity over 100 cycles. In contrast, the Na<sub>2</sub>NC/G anode can still deliver a high capacity of 207 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, which corresponding to a capacity retention of 92% over 100 cycles, suggesting an excellent cycling stability of the graphene-wrapped Na<sub>2</sub>NC nanoflowers. A comparison of the charge/discharge behaviors of the Na<sub>2</sub>NC/G electrode with those of reported organic Na-storage anodes is given in Table S1 of the Supporting Information. The stable electrochemical performances of Na<sub>2</sub>NC/G anode is apparently attributed to the 3D graphene matrix that provides not only highly conductive channels for electron transportation but also a buffering effect for releasing the mechanical stress of the material due to Na-ion insertion/extraction.</p>
         
         <p>To get an insight into the redox mechanism of the organic anode, the bonding states and the Na content of Na<sub>2</sub>NC molecules in the anode cycled at different charge/discharge states were examined by IR and inductively coupled plasma (ICP) analysis, respectively. As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll201502278-fig-0003">3</a>, when the Na<sub>2</sub>NC/G anode was fully discharged from its open-circuit potential to 0.1 V, the characteristic vibrations (1560 and 1412 cm<sup>−1</sup>) of carboxylic groups <span class="icomoon"></span>COO<sup>−</sup> became weak and shifted to lower wave numbers, while the shoulder band at 1605, 1495, and 1395 cm<sup>−1</sup>, which are due to the vibrations of CC bond for naphthalene rings completely disappeared, indicating an electrochemical enolization reaction of the carboxylic groups, which leads to the reduction of the CO bonds and a simultaneous rearrangement of CC bonds. On the reversed charge, all the IR features recovered their intensities and positions at the full charge, suggesting a structural and electrochemical reversibility of Na<sub>2</sub>NC molecules. Since the reversible enolization reaction of carboxylic groups must involve reversible association/disassociation of electrolyte cations with/from the oxygen atoms of carboxylic groups for charge counterbalance,<span><a href="#smll201502278-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201502278-bib-0008_R_d56705321e686" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Energy &amp; Environ. Sci. Adv. Energy Mater.">8</a>, <a href="#smll201502278-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201502278-bib-0009_R_d56705321e686" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Mater.">9</a></span> the redox reaction of Na<sub>2</sub>NC molecules can be regarded as reversible Na<sup>+</sup>-insertion/extraction reactions into/from the organic framework as shown in <b>Scheme</b> <a href="#smll201502278-fig-0004">1</a>.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll201502278-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9f38e561-a2a9-466c-991c-e43831c37544/smll201502278-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/9f38e561-a2a9-466c-991c-e43831c37544/smll201502278-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/8c4aa251-1835-4c2f-9263-64c17a663e6e/smll201502278-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/9f38e561-a2a9-466c-991c-e43831c37544/smll201502278-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201502278-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201502278" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) A typical charge/discharge profile of the Na<sub>2</sub>NC/G anode, where Arabic characters on the curves denote the sampling positions and b) changes in the IR spectrums of the Na<sub>2</sub>NC/G electrodes at different depths of charge and discharge.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll201502278-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/415fa7f1-296c-4fa9-aff3-56801e0bd4ac/smll201502278-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/415fa7f1-296c-4fa9-aff3-56801e0bd4ac/smll201502278-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/85e066a6-38ac-4500-82ea-192a712424b3/smll201502278-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/415fa7f1-296c-4fa9-aff3-56801e0bd4ac/smll201502278-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Scheme 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201502278-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201502278" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The 2-electron redox mechanism of Na<sub>2</sub>NC molecules.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll201502278-sec-0020">
         
         <p>Quantitative analysis of the Na contents in the cycled Na<sub>2</sub>NC/G anode provides a direct evidence for the Na<sup>+</sup>-insertion mechanism. <b>Table</b> <a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#smll201502278-tbl-0001">1</a> gives the Na contents in the cycled Na<sub>2</sub>NC/G anodes at different charge/discharge states. As is shown in the table, the Na content in this organic anode increased from 17.7% to 36.45 wt% when discharged from its open-circuit potential to a terminating potential of 0.1 V, corresponding to 4.12 Na<sup>+</sup> ions inserted in each Na<sub>2</sub>NC unit, which suggests a full utilization of the carbonyl groups to host Na<sup>+</sup> ions. Meanwhile, when fully recharged back to a positive potential of 2.5 V, the Na content recovered to its original value of 17.7 wt%, indicating a complete extraction of the inserted Na<sup>+</sup> ions from the Na<sub>2</sub>NC anode. In other words, the Na<sub>2</sub>NC molecules function as an organic host to reversibly accommodate Na<sup>+</sup> ions, delivering a two electron redox capacity at very suitable anodic potentials. The morphological changes of the Na<sub>2</sub>NC electrode cycled at 20 mA g<sup>−1</sup> were also characterized by SEM microscopy. As shown in Figure S9 of the Supporting Information, though the particle size slightly decreased due to the partial exfoliation of the nanospheres, the Na<sub>2</sub>NC particles on the cycled electrode still kept a nanospherical shape even after 100 cycles, demonstrating a considerable morphological stability of Na<sub>2</sub>NC as an anode material.</p>
         
         <div class="article-table-content" id="smll201502278-tbl-0001">
            <header class="article-table-caption"><span class="table-caption__label">Table 1.

         </div>
         
         <p>In summary, we introduced a small organic disodium salt (Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>12</sub>H<sub>6</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, Na<sub>2</sub>NC) as a novel organic anode material for Na-ion batteries. To overcome the issues of poor electronic conductivity and sluggish electrochemical kinetics of the organic compound, we synthesized a graphene/Na<sub>2</sub>NC nanocomposite (Na<sub>2</sub>NC/G), where the porous Na<sub>2</sub>NC nanoflowers were embedded in highly conductive graphene nanosheets. Due to its high interfacial surface areas, abundant electronic and ionic transport channels, the as-prepared Na<sub>2</sub>NC/G material demonstrates a high reversible capacity of &gt;220 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, a strong rate capability at 10 C rate and a cycling stability with 92% capacity retention over 100 cycles, offering a low cost and sustainable anode for Na-ion batteries. In addition, the nanoarchitecture and its facile ultrasonic synthesis described in this work may be easily extended to a wide variety of redox-active organic molecules to promote their applications in new energy technologies.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll201502278-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll201502278-sec-0030-title"> Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <p><i>Materials Synthesis and Characterization</i>: 0.216 g (1 mmol) 2,6-Naphthalenedicarboxylic acid (H<sub>2</sub>C<sub>12</sub>H<sub>6</sub>O<sub>4</sub> (H<sub>2</sub>NC), Aldrich, 98%) was reacted with 0.12g (3 mmol) sodium hydroxide powders (Aldrich, 99%) in 20 mL methanol under magnetic stirring at 80 °C for 24 h. After cooled to room temperature, the precipitate was vacuum filtered, and dried in vacuum at 60 °C overnight (yield: 95%). To obtain NF-Na<sub>2</sub>NC, 0.1 g MF-Na<sub>2</sub>NC powder was first ultrasonically treated in 10 mL absolute methanol for 10 min and then vacuum distilled to remove the solvent. Repeating this procedure for five times and finally drying the product in vacuum at 60 °C overnight gave a pale yellow NF-Na<sub>2</sub>NC powder. Na2NC/G composite was prepared by adding 0.1 g NF-Na<sub>2</sub>NC and 0.005 g (5% by wt) graphene nanosheet (Xian Feng nano Mstar Technology Ltd., Nanjing, China) into 10 mL methanol, and then ultrasonically treated for 30 min. The final composite powder was obtained by removing the solvent and vacuum dried at 60 °C overnight. The Fourier transform infrared spectra (FTIR) was recorded using KBr pellets on AVATAR 360 spectrometer (Nicolet Instrument Corp., USA) in wavelength range of 400–4000 cm<sup>−1</sup>. X-ray powder diffraction patterns were obtained on a Shimadzu XRD-6000 diffractometer with Cu Kα. The diffraction data was recorded in the 2θ range of 10°–80° with a scan rate of 2° min<sup>−1</sup>. <sup>1</sup>H and <sup>13</sup>C NMR spectra were recorded in D<sub>2</sub>O on AVANCE III 400 (1H 400 MHz, 13C 100 MHz). The TGA measurement was conducted on a TGA Q500 thermogravimetric analyzer (TA Instrument, USA) in air at a heating rate of 10 °C min<sup>−1</sup> from room temperature to 900 °C. N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption isotherms was measured at liquid nitrogen temperature using ASAP 2020. The morphologies of the materials were observed by using SEM (Sirion, 2000, FEI) and TEM (JEM-2010FEF). ICP results were obtained on a Thermo Fisher Scientific iCAP6300 ICP spectrometer. For ICP analysis of Na content, the electrode samples at different charge and discharge states taken out from the disassembled cells and rinsed with pure diethyl carbonate solvent for several times and then burnt in a tubular furnace at 550 °C for 10 h. The as-obtained sodium oxide residue was then dissolved in HCl for the atomic emission analysis using IRIS Intrepid XPS spectrometer.</p>
         
         <p><i>Electrochemical Measurements</i>: The charge–discharge performances were examined using 2016 coin-type cells with the Na<sub>2</sub>NC-based materials as working electrode and a Na disk as counter electrode. The Na<sub>2</sub>NC anodes were prepared by rolling a mixture of 70 wt% of Na<sub>2</sub>NC or Na<sub>2</sub>NC/G composite, 20 wt% of supper P and 10% polytetrafluoroethylene (PTFE) into a thin film, then pressing a piece of film (0.8 m<sup>2</sup>) onto a Al mesh. The SP/graphene electrode was prepared by adding 90 wt% SP/graphene (5/1, m/m) mixture and 10 wt% PTFE, then rolling them into a thin film and pressing a piece of film (0.8 m<sup>2</sup>) onto an Al mesh. The electrodes were dried at 80 °C under vacuum for 12 h. A typical loading of active mass is 2 mg cm<sup>−2</sup>. The electrolyte was 1.0 mol L<sup>−1</sup> NaPF<sub>6</sub> dissolved in ethylene carbonate/diethyl carbonate (EC/DEC, 1:1 by vol.) solution. All the cells were assembled in a glove box with water/oxygen content lower than 1 ppm and tested at room temperature. The galvanostatic discharging–charging tests were conducted on a LAND cycler (Wuhan Kingnuo Electronic Co.,China) at a potential range of 0.1–2.5 V. Cyclic voltammetric measurements of MF-Na<sub>2</sub>NC and NF-Na<sub>2</sub>NC were performed at a scan rate of 0.05 mV s<sup>−1</sup> at a potential range of 0.02–2.5 V on a CHI 660a electrochemical workstation (ChenHua Instruments Co., China).</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">

               <div class="support-info__table-wrapper article-table-content-wrapper">
                  <p>As a service to our authors and readers, this journal provides supporting information supplied by the authors. Such materials are peer reviewed and may be re-organized for online delivery, but are not copy-edited or typeset. Technical support issues arising from supporting information (other than missing files) should be addressed to the authors.</p>
